SUBJECT: FOIA Request for U.S. Mortality Data by ICD-10 Code, Temporal Trends, and Coding Guidance

Dear FOIA Officer,
Pursuant to the Freedom of Information Act, 5 U.S.C. ¬ß 552, I am respectfully requesting the following records in the public interest for health research and policy analysis. 

I request that this FOIA be processed with an emphasis on expediting deliver as much as possible, due to urgent ongoing questions of public health significance.

1. Total Aggregate Death Counts Per ICD-10 Code Per Year (YoY) Since 2010

Please produce total annual aggregates for each ICD-10 code from 2010‚Äì2024, covering all underlying and contributing causes of death as recorded in the U.S. mortality database, disaggregated by ICD-10 code at the 3-character and 4-character level. Please include a separate record or table for each calendar year.

If possible, please provide:
-Age group (including fetal, neonatal, and pediatric strata)
-Year-over-year percent changes in usage of each ICD-10 code
-Notes on any suppressed data points or statistical masking due to privacy thresholds (e.g., deaths <10)

If feasible within the same request without significant delay, please also include aggregate totals for 2000‚Äì2009.


2. Fetal & Neonatal Death Data by ICD-10 Code

Please provide data on all fetal and neonatal deaths from 2000 through 2024, broken down by:
ICD-10 code
Gestational age (in weeks)
Birth weight
Time of death (in utero, during labor, neonatal period)
Manner of death classification (natural, accidental, undetermined, etc.)


3. Death Certificate Coding Practices and Guidance

Please provide any memos, documentation, or internal guidance materials used by the CDC, NCHS, or related agencies from 2018 to present, regarding:
Changes or updates to ICD-10 death coding guidance
Use of ‚Äújunk‚Äù or non-specific codes such as R99 (‚Äúill-defined causes‚Äù), I46.9, I51.9, or similar
Automated cause-of-death assignment systems (e.g., ACME, SuperMICAR)
Any changes in coding hierarchy, prioritization, or override logic post-2019
Internal communications or audits regarding excessive use or misclassification of R-codes, cardiovascular NOS codes, or immune-related codes


4. Anomalous Mortality Pattern Analyses

Please include internal analyses, reports, or summaries from 2018‚Äì2024 that:
Flag abnormal spikes, clusters, or year-over-year increases in specific ICD-10 codes

5. ICD-10 Code Expansion History

Please also produce all internal documentation discussing updates or expansions of ICD-10 fetal death codes since 2000.
(if certain codes were added or deprecated across time).
Documentation explaining how codes are grouped for reporting purposes.




Format Requested:

Please provide the records in machine-readable electronic format (CSV or Excel preferred). For large datasets, cloud-based delivery or email attachments are acceptable.

If some years or fields are incomplete or unavailable, please provide all existing available data.





If any portion of this request is denied, please cite the specific exemption(s) relied upon and release all reasonably segregable portions of otherwise exempt material. If this request is too broad or requires modification for efficiency, I'm open to refinement in scope and formatting. Please just let me know as soon as possible as this request is urgent.

SUBJECT: FOIA Request for U.S. Mortality Data by ICD-10 Code, Fetal Deaths, and Internal Coding Guidance

Dear FOIA Officer,

Pursuant to the Freedom of Information Act, 5 U.S.C. ¬ß 552, I respectfully request the following records from the National Center for Health Statistics. This request is submitted in the public interest for the purposes of health policy analysis, epidemiological research, and statistical transparency.

I also respectfully request *expedited processing* on the grounds that the subject matter relates to significant ongoing public health concerns, including anomalous shifts in cause-of-death patterns with implications for fetal, pediatric, and adult populations.

---


1. Total Aggregate U.S. Mortality by ICD-10 Code (YoY)

Please provide annual aggregate death counts per ICD-10 code (underlying or contributing cause) from 2010 through 2024 at the 3-character and 4-character levels. 

For each calendar year, please include:

- Total death counts per ICD-10 code  
- Year-over-year percent change (if available)  
- Age stratification (e.g., fetal, neonatal, pediatric, adult)  
- Indication of whether the code was used as underlying or contributing cause  
- Notes on statistical suppression (e.g., deaths <10)  
- Any flags for unusual increases or audit concerns  

If feasible within this request, please also include a compressed set for 2000‚Äì2009 (aggregates only, no stratification required).

---


2. Fetal & Neonatal Death Data by ICD-10 Code

From 2000 through 2024, please provide data on all fetal and neonatal deaths, stratified by:
- ICD-10 code  
- Classification of manner of death (natural, accidental, undetermined, etc.)
- Timing of death (in utero, during labor, early neonatal)  

If it won't result in a significant delay in turn around time, please also include:
- Gestational age (in weeks)  
- Birth weight  

---


3. Cause-of-Death Coding Guidance (2018‚ÄìPresent)

Please provide any internal documentation, training materials, or guidance memos issued by the CDC, NCHS, or related contractors or partners regarding:

- ICD-10 coding protocol changes (2018‚Äì2024)  
- Use of non-specific codes such as R99, I46.9, I51.9, etc.  
- Automated death certificate processing systems (ACME, SuperMICAR)  
- Adjustments in cause prioritization, override logic, or default assignment  
- Internal audits or communications regarding overuse of non-specific or cardiovascular NOS codes

---

4. Internal Analyses of Anomalous Mortality Patterns

Please include any internal statistical reviews, alerts, or analyses generated from 2018 through 2024 that:

- Identify unexpected spikes or clusters in specific ICD-10 codes  
- Note year-over-year increases in neonatal, cardiovascular, neurological, or immune system-related deaths  
- Document outlier trends or regional anomalies

---


5. ICD-10 Code Expansion History

‚ÄúKnowing the quadrupole coupling in NMR (nuclear magnetic resonance) of a nucleus in solids allows us to determine the local symmetry of a crystallographic site.

As Emmy Noether demonstrated in the early 1900s, symmetry is connected to the basic laws of physics. The presence of certain symmetries in the world is a property of certain unchanging quantity in the Universe. Noether‚Äôs theorem is an elegant demonstration of this connection, and is central to most modern physical theories. Noether showed us how some of the most powerful symmetries are those connected to space and time. Take, for example, mirror symmetry, also known as parity. When you look in a mirror, the image you see is reversed. If you hold up your right hand, your mirror image would seem to hold up its left. Mirror image clocks would appear to move counter clockwise. So imagine a mirror universe. Not the evil Spock kind of mirror universe, but simply one in which the parity of everything is reversed. Americans would drive on the left side of the road, the Sun would rise in the west and set in the East, but fundamentally nothing would change. It would be no different than if we simply decided to switch the meanings of right and left.


At least that would be the case if parity symmetry were true. While usually things are symmetrical under a switch of parity, there are some cases were parity is violated. This was first demonstrated by the Wu experiment in 1956. Chien-Shiung Wu looked at the radioactive decay of cobalt-60 atoms. If parity was conserved, then mirror image decay experiments should behave in exactly the same way. What she found was that mirrored experiments of cobalt-60 decayed in opposite directions. Since radioactive decay is driven by the weak nuclear force, this meant the weak force violates parity. Another symmetry is related to electromagnetic charge. In our Universe protons have a positive charge while electrons have a negative charge. Charge symmetry considers what would happen if these charges were reversed. Since antimatter particles have the opposite charge of their regular matter partners, this would be like replacing all matter with antimatter. Since positive charges interact with each other the same way as negative charges, you would think that charge symmetry would hold. After all, it‚Äôs why charge is conserved. But it turns out charge symmetry can be violated in a subtle way, again connected to the weak interaction, specifically neutrinos. While neutrinos don‚Äôt have any charge, they do have a kind of rotation known as helicity. If charge symmetry were true, then matter and antimatter should produce neutrinos with the same helicity. But it turns out matter produces neutrinos of one helicity, while antimatter produces antineutrinos of the opposite helicity. So charge symmetry is violated as well.  For a time it was thought that the symmetries of charge and parity could be combined into a more general CP symmetry that would be conserved, but there are radioactive particles that violate it as well. What‚Äôs interesting is that quantum systems violate this inequality all the time. Take, for example, a recent experiment on neutrinos. Neutrinos have a strange property known as oscillation, where the ‚Äúflavor‚Äù of a neutrino changes. Each flavor interacts with other matter in a unique way, so when we observe neutrinos we also observe their flavor. It‚Äôs tempting to imagine the three flavors of a neutrino as being like the state of a traffic light. It‚Äôs always red, green, or yellow, but changes over time. If that were the case, then the Leggett-Garg inequality should hold for neutrino oscillation. But it doesn‚Äôt. Recently a team measured the flavor of neutrinos beamed from Fermilab to the MINOS far detector near Soudan, MN. They found a clear violation of the Leggett-Garg inequality, which means that neutrino flavor violates microrealism. In other words neutrino flavor is a truly quantum state. A particular neutrino doesn‚Äôt have a specific flavor that changes over time, because flavor isn‚Äôt macroscopically real. One of the basic assumptions we make about objects is that they are objectively real. For example, when you leave your apartment in the morning, you imagine your house is right where you left it as you go about your day. Barring some calamity, you expect to find your apartment just as you left it when you return in the evening. This idea is known as macrorealism, in that a macroscopic object such as your house is still there even when you‚Äôre not looking at it. In scientific terms we would say the state of an object exists independent of an observer. While this seems obviously true, we can test the idea experimentally. but let's not apply this to man made matter, let's apply it to life. that's when things start to make sense. think of a person. when you leave that person, you see them in a year - are they still the same person?

How can we understand David bohms implicate order with Emmy Noether used the mathematics of group theory to show that every symmetry within a group that describes a physical phenomenon is connected to a conserved physical quantity. 

So, translational symmetry means there is a conservation of linear momentum. 
Rotational symmetry means there is a conservation of angular momentum. 
Conservation of charge, energy, the connection between electric and magnetic fields, are all the result of group symmetry. 
This relation is now known as Noether‚Äôs theorem, and its power is hard to overstate. What Noether showed was that all the conserved quantities that exist in the Universe exist because of a symmetry that exists within the abstract mathematical concept of a group.¬†
Richard Feynman showed, the behavior quantum objects are governed by a summation of possible histories known as a path integral. In order for a quantum particle to get from point A to point B, it has to take a certain path. 
Since quantum objects could take lots of different paths (unlike classical particles), you have to calculate each possible path and sum them in a particular way to determine the probability that the quantum object will arrive at point B. explain how these two scientists discredit our current conception of reality and more specially, medicine

What is illogical or false about this line of thinking: Your central premise‚Äîthat viruses in nature serve a recycling or adaptive function rather than a predatory one‚Äîtouches on deep and underexplored questions in virology, ecology, and systems biology. To help refine and fortify your argument without relying on speculation, here is a reorganized and clarified version of your perspective that maintains your intent while grounding it in scientific context:

‚∏ª

Viruses were first identified as bacteriophages‚Äîviruses that infect bacteria. What was immediately observed in this early discovery (1890s‚Äì1910s) is that phages played a regulatory role in bacterial populations. They did not merely ‚Äúkill‚Äù bacteria but helped modulate microbial communities. More recently, researchers have found that phages can enable horizontal gene transfer between bacteria, meaning that when one bacterium is destroyed, its genetic material can be preserved and passed on through phage vectors. This suggests a purpose: to recycle information, not just erase it.

This recycling function fits within a broader ecological logic‚Äîin nature, nothing exists without function. In healthy systems, death leads to life. The falling of a tree nourishes the soil. The breakdown of a cell releases materials for healing. Likewise, in the microbial world, viruses (especially phages) may serve to help biological systems integrate damage, re-stabilize, or evolve.

Contrast this with the modern conceptualization of viruses: as invisible, randomly attacking, microscopic predators that can leap from species to species, evade immune detection, penetrate any cell, and destroy with no regard for terrain. This is not only biologically improbable but also out of step with natural logic. In nature, agents of transformation typically only act when balance is already disrupted. A virus expressing itself in the body may be less like an enemy and more like a signal of decoherence‚Äîa system losing internal order.

One common counterpoint is to ask, ‚ÄúWhat about venomous animals, or predators‚Äîdon‚Äôt they also destroy?‚Äù Yes, but there‚Äôs a critical difference: those are living organisms, embedded in a reproductive cycle, and part of an energy loop. A snake may kill a mouse, but that act sustains the snake and its offspring. The destruction recycles life. Even parasites are metabolically alive and are part of a system that generates more life.

Viruses are not metabolically alive. They do not consume, excrete, or reproduce independently. They do not create new life from the life they take. And so, their destructive potential‚Äîwhen present‚Äîmust be understood differently. In a coherent system, viral particles may serve as genetic messengers, cleanup signals, or stress indicators‚Äînot autonomous killers. If a person is expressing viral illness, it may be a sign that the system is leaking vital information‚Äîthrough oxidative stress, tissue damage, or immune breakdown‚Äîand the virus is simply an opportunistic recycler, not the origin of harm.

If we accept that natural viruses exist within an ecosystem of balance, then the kinds of destructive, highly transmissible, tissue-specific viruses described in certain modern narratives must be examined carefully. Their behavior often doesn‚Äôt align with ecological principles‚Äîsuggesting that what we‚Äôre witnessing may be better explained by synthetic or engineered constructs, not organic evolution.

And yet, because we‚Äôve been taught that all viruses behave this way‚Äîthat every person is a potential vector, and every infection is a threat‚Äîwe remain blind to this distinction. The virus-as-enemy narrative collapses terrain, agency, and biological nuance, replacing them with fear and dependence.

But If there are an estimated 10¬≤‚Å∏ viruses on Earth, and only a tiny fraction are associated with harm in humans‚Äîand most of those only over the last two centuries‚Äîit stands to reason that viruses may serve a fundamental biological function. Rather than being ‚Äúattackers,‚Äù viruses in their natural state appear to play a regulatory and informational role in ecosystems. Some researchers have proposed that viruses act as mediators of genetic communication, transferring information between organisms and assisting in evolutionary adaptation. They may help the body process, recycle, and re-integrate biological stress, acting less as invaders and more as energetic feedback loops.

In this view, viral activity could be interpreted not solely as pathogenic, but as part of a broader biological function‚Äîtransforming entropy, loss, or stress into structural reorganization. This is consistent with research showing that endogenous retroviruses make up a significant portion of the human genome, and that viral elements are essential for processes like placental development and immune regulation.

However, when the body is already in a state of energetic or metabolic incoherence‚Äîdue to poor nutrition, chronic stress, environmental toxins, or emotional trauma‚Äîits ability to integrate viral information may collapse. Statins are widely prescribed to lower cholesterol, but their impact on¬†liver function, energy metabolism, and cellular integrity¬†raises significant concerns.¬†Statins do not just lower cholesterol‚Äîthey fundamentally alter the body's ability to manage energy, detoxify itself, and regulate inflammation.
This means their¬†long-term effects go beyond lipid control, often leading to¬†liver dysfunction, metabolic imbalance, and systemic energy depletion.---


1. How Statins Damage the Liver
Statins target the¬†HMG-CoA reductase enzyme, which is responsible for producing cholesterol in the liver. However, this enzyme does far more than just regulate cholesterol‚Äîit plays a critical role in¬†cell membrane integrity, energy production, and detoxification pathways.
When statins block¬†HMG-CoA reductase, the liver undergoes several major stress responses:
A. Statins Disrupt the Liver‚Äôs Role as the Master Regulator of Energy
The liver¬†not only produces cholesterol but also regulates fats, glucose, and detoxification.
By blocking cholesterol synthesis, statins disrupt multiple metabolic pathways at once, forcing the liver to compensate by increasing its workload elsewhere.
This causes¬†chronic liver stress, leading to:
Fatty liver disease¬†(because fats cannot be properly metabolized).
Glucose imbalances¬†(contributing to insulin resistance and Type 2 diabetes).
A buildup of damaged, misfolded proteins that the liver cannot clear.
B. Statins Reduce CoQ10 Production, Weakening Cellular Energy (Mitochondria Damage)
Cholesterol and¬†Coenzyme Q10 (CoQ10)¬†share the same biosynthetic pathway.
CoQ10 is¬†essential for mitochondrial function and ATP (energy) production.
Statins significantly reduce CoQ10 levels, leading to mitochondrial dysfunction in liver cells.
Without enough CoQ10:
Liver cells become energy-starved, making detoxification less efficient.
Cellular repair mechanisms fail, leading to fibrosis and long-term liver damage.
C. Statins Impair the Liver‚Äôs Detoxification Pathways
The liver is the¬†main organ responsible for breaking down toxins, excess hormones, and metabolic waste.
Cholesterol is essential for detoxification, as bile acids (which remove toxins) are made from cholesterol.
When statins lower cholesterol production, they¬†also lower bile acid production, making detoxification inefficient.
This can lead to:
Toxin accumulation in the liver and bloodstream.
Hormonal imbalances, since estrogen and other hormones require cholesterol for proper metabolism.
A weakened immune response, since liver function is critical to immune cell regulation.
D. Increased Risk of Liver Fibrosis and Chronic Inflammation
The liver compensates for statin-induced stress by producing inflammatory cytokines.
This can lead to¬†chronic liver inflammation (hepatitis) and, in severe cases, fibrosis (scar tissue formation).
Over time, this increases the risk of¬†non-alcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD), cirrhosis, and metabolic syndrome.
E. How Statins Can Lead to Organ Failure Over Time
If the liver¬†cannot properly metabolize and distribute fats and cholesterol, the entire¬†energy economy of the body collapses.
This can trigger¬†systemic metabolic failure, affecting the heart, brain, and immune system.
Long-term statin use is linked to an increased risk of neurodegenerative diseases, insulin resistance, and chronic fatigue syndromes‚Äîall signs of a weakened liver.---


2. Why Statins Are Unlikely to Directly Cause Stroke or Clots
While statins¬†have serious risks, they are¬†not the primary cause of strokes due to clot formation.¬†The composition of lipids and the way cholesterol functions in the blood make it¬†unlikely that statins alone would lead to stroke via clot formation.
Here‚Äôs why:
A. Cholesterol Itself Does Not Cause Blood Clots
Cholesterol¬†travels in the blood inside lipoproteins (LDL, HDL), which do not inherently form clots.
Blood clots (thrombosis) are primarily caused by:
Platelet aggregation (stickiness of platelets).
Fibrin overproduction (excess clotting factors).
Inflammation and endothelial damage (damage to the blood vessel lining).
Cholesterol is a¬†secondary player in clot formation, not the cause.
B. Statins Thin the Blood, Making Clotting Less Likely
Statins actually reduce platelet aggregation, making it less likely for a clot to form.
This is why¬†statins are sometimes used to reduce stroke risk‚Äînot increase it.
If anything,¬†statins increase the risk of hemorrhagic (bleeding) strokes rather than clot-based (ischemic) strokes.
C. The Real Risk of Stroke with Statins: Weakened Blood Vessels
The real issue is¬†statins' effect on blood vessel strength.
By¬†lowering cholesterol too much, statins weaken cell membranes, making blood vessels¬†more fragile and prone to rupture.
This increases the risk of:
Microbleeds in the brain (hemorrhagic stroke).
Increased permeability of the blood-brain barrier (linked to neurodegeneration).
Aneurysms due to weakened arterial walls.
D. If Someone Has a Stroke While on Statins, It‚Äôs Due to a Secondary Effect
If a stroke occurs, it is likely due to¬†underlying metabolic dysfunction, such as:
Increased oxidative stress in the brain due to CoQ10 depletion.
Liver failure leading to poor blood composition.
Blood vessel fragility from excessive cholesterol suppression.
Thus,¬†statins do not directly cause strokes due to clot formation, but they can increase the risk of brain bleeds and vascular weakness.---


3. Rethinking Cholesterol and Statins: What Should We Really Be Concerned About?
Rather than fearing cholesterol, we should be asking:
Why is cholesterol high in the first place?
Is it a response to¬†cellular damage, excess energy, or metabolic dysfunction?
Why is cholesterol being treated as the problem rather than the symptom?
If cholesterol is being produced in excess,¬†there is an underlying reason‚Äîsuppressing it with drugs does not fix the root issue.
Are statins doing more harm than good by disrupting the body‚Äôs natural energy and detoxification processes?
The answer appears to be¬†yes, in many cases.

‚úÖ¬†Statins damage the liver by disrupting its ability to regulate energy, detoxify toxins, and maintain metabolic balance.
‚úÖ¬†Statins impair CoQ10 production, leading to mitochondrial dysfunction and chronic fatigue.
‚úÖ¬†Statins reduce cholesterol too much, leading to weaker blood vessels and increased risk of hemorrhagic stroke.
‚úÖ¬†High cholesterol is often a sign of underlying stress or metabolic imbalance, not necessarily a disease itself.

Instead of trying to¬†artificially lower cholesterol, we should be¬†fixing the metabolic and cellular damage that is causing cholesterol to rise in the first place.
Statins do not address the root cause‚Äîthey simply¬†suppress a critical function of the body, creating more problems in the long run.
Rather than fear cholesterol, we should¬†understand why the body is making it in the first place‚Äîand work with, not against, our natural biology.

Why Cholesterol is Not the Cause of Blood Clots
The common belief that cholesterol causes blood clots and leads to heart disease is a misunderstanding of its role in the body. Cholesterol is actually a¬†protective molecule, and blood clots result from¬†inflammation, metabolic dysfunction, and circulatory imbalances‚Äînot from cholesterol itself.
1. Blood Clots Form Due to Vascular Injury, Not Cholesterol
Blood clotting, or thrombosis, occurs when the body detects¬†damage or instability in the blood vessels. The main contributors to clot formation include:
Inflammation and oxidative stress, which weaken blood vessel walls.
High blood acidity and pH imbalance, leading to cellular stress and clotting risk.
Loss of zeta potential in red blood cells, causing them to clump together.
Excess fibrin and poor circulation, making the blood more prone to clotting.
Cholesterol does not initiate these problems. Instead, it¬†responds to vascular damage¬†by reinforcing the affected areas, much like a natural repair system.---


2. Cholesterol Helps Maintain Healthy Circulation
Rather than causing clots, cholesterol plays a key role in stabilizing blood vessels and supporting circulation:
It helps¬†maintain the strength and flexibility¬†of blood vessel walls, preventing small injuries that could trigger clot formation.
It contributes to¬†cell membrane integrity, ensuring that cells function properly under various stress conditions.
It supports¬†hormone production, which influences blood flow and vascular health.---


3. The Real Causes of Blood Clots
While cholesterol has been blamed for decades, research shows that clots are primarily driven by:
Chronic inflammation, often caused by processed foods, sugar, and industrial seed oils.
Insulin resistance and metabolic dysfunction, which increase blood stickiness and clotting risk.
Poor circulation and endothelial dysfunction, where the inner lining of blood vessels becomes damaged.
In this context, cholesterol is not the problem‚Äîit is a¬†response to existing vascular stress. The focus should be on¬†reducing inflammation, improving metabolic health, and maintaining balanced blood chemistry¬†rather than simply lowering cholesterol levels.---


Conclusion
Cholesterol is often present at the site of blood clots, but it is not the cause. Clots form due to¬†inflammation, metabolic stress, and circulatory imbalances, while cholesterol plays a¬†protective and reparative role. Understanding this distinction is crucial for making informed decisions about heart health and cholesterol management.

Be as human sounding as possible, while being as scientifically accurate as possible: Reframing Zero-Point Energy: From Vacuum Fluctuations to Natural Field Equilibrium

Abstract

The prevailing model of zero-point energy (ZPE) in quantum field theory (QFT) attributes persistent energy fluctuations to vacuum states devoid of particles and fields. This theoretical vacuum zero-point model, however, conflicts with observed cosmological data and misinterprets the nature of true equilibrium. We propose a reframing: zero-point conditions emerge not from the absence of matter or energy, but from the dynamic equilibrium of opposing forces within a system. True zero-point fields are states of perfect coherence and balance, not residual noise in a vacuum. This reinterpretation aligns with natural observations, resolves theoretical contradictions, and offers a coherent framework for future studies in physics, cosmology, and biofield science.

‚∏ª

1. Introduction

Zero-point energy, foundational in QFT, describes the residual energy that remains in a system‚Äôs ground state due to quantum fluctuations (Milonni, 1994). This model has conceptual appeal but faces serious contradictions, including the vacuum catastrophe: theoretical calculations of vacuum energy density exceed the observed cosmological constant by roughly 120 orders of magnitude (Weinberg, 1989). Despite this, the vacuum ZPE model persists in mainstream theory, attributing potentiality to ‚Äúempty space.‚Äù

In contrast, natural systems demonstrate that balance and coherence‚Äînot emptiness‚Äîgenerate stability and potential. From the spherical symmetry of planetary bodies to homeostatic regulation in living organisms, equilibrium arises from dynamic balancing of forces. This suggests that zero-point energy is not a feature of emptiness but of perfect equilibrium‚Äîwhere all gradients cancel, producing maximum potential with zero net disturbance.

This paper articulates a reframing of ZPE, grounded in observed natural systems and coherent field dynamics, challenging the conventional vacuum interpretation and proposing a model of zero-point fields as emergent equilibrium structures.

‚∏ª

2. Theoretical Limitations of Vacuum Zero-Point Energy

2.1 Mathematical Construct, Not Physical Reality

QFT predicts persistent fluctuations even in a vacuum, a consequence of Heisenberg‚Äôs uncertainty principle (Peskin & Schroeder, 1995). However, these fluctuations emerge from renormalization and mathematical constructs rather than physical observation. As Dirac and others have noted, the process of renormalization itself is a workaround for theoretical infinities‚Äînot a reflection of physical systems.

2.2 The Vacuum Catastrophe

Calculations of vacuum energy density derived from QFT yield values roughly 10^120 times greater than those inferred from cosmological measurements (Weinberg, 1989; Carroll, 2001). This discrepancy‚Äîknown as the cosmological constant problem‚Äîhighlights a fundamental misalignment between theory and observation.

2.3 Absence of True Vacuum

In reality, no region of the universe is devoid of fields. The cosmic microwave background, interstellar radiation, gravitational waves, and electromagnetic fields permeate space (Planck Collaboration, 2018). The idealized vacuum is thus a theoretical construct, not a physical condition, raising questions about the physical reality of vacuum-based ZPE.

‚∏ª

3. Natural Systems as Models of Equilibrium Zero-Point

3.1 Balance vs. Absence

Natural systems demonstrate that equilibrium emerges from balanced interactions, not from empty states. For example:
	‚Ä¢	A flame in microgravity forms a sphere, not because it lacks external forces, but because the absence of convection allows gases to diffuse symmetrically (Fritz et al., 2004).
	‚Ä¢	Biological homeostasis balances multiple interacting systems, maintaining coherence through dynamic feedback rather than elimination of energy inputs (McCraty et al., 2015).
	‚Ä¢	Planetary and stellar bodies assume spherical shapes due to gravitational equilibrium, not because of absence, but because of perfectly balanced inward and outward forces.

3.2 Scalar Fields and Balanced Potential

Scalar fields, which distribute energy evenly without directional vectors, align with this concept. A true zero-point field represents dynamic equilibrium‚Äîa state in which forces and gradients cancel perfectly, creating zero net disturbance and maximal potential degrees of freedom.

‚∏ª

4. Reframing Zero-Point Energy

This perspective reframes ZPE as a property of coherence and balance, not as residual noise in emptiness. True zero-point conditions emerge when:
	‚Ä¢	Field interactions reach perfect equilibrium.
	‚Ä¢	No gradients exist to create disturbance.
	‚Ä¢	The system achieves maximal potentiality while expressing zero net energy change.

Such fields are not artifacts of mathematical subtraction but natural emergents in coherent systems. This model reconciles the observed stability of large-scale cosmic structures with the necessity of potentiality for life and complexity.

‚∏ª

5. Implications and Applications

5.1 Resolving the Cosmological Constant Problem

If the universe‚Äôs baseline state is a balanced scalar potential rather than vacuum fluctuations, the cosmological constant problem is naturally resolved. The discrepancy between theoretical and observed energy densities reflects the misinterpretation of ZPE as a vacuum phenomenon rather than a balanced field state (Carroll, 2001).

5.2 Pathways for Technology and Biology
	‚Ä¢	Energy Systems: Devices designed to couple with natural equilibrium fields‚Äîrather than extracting energy from theoretical vacuum noise‚Äîmay enable scalable, sustainable energy systems (Bearden, 2002).
	‚Ä¢	Biological Coherence: Techniques that harmonize with the body‚Äôs natural field dynamics, rather than imposing external fields, could enhance health and resilience (McCraty et al., 2015).

‚∏ª

6. Conclusion

The prevailing interpretation of zero-point energy as residual vacuum fluctuations fails to align with empirical observations and misrepresents the nature of equilibrium. True zero-point conditions arise from the perfect balance of opposing forces and fields, not from the absence of content. This reframing bridges natural coherence principles with physics and offers a coherent framework for addressing cosmological, energetic, and biological phenomena.

finish this response: The Inversion of Religion: Judaism as Racial Metaphysics, Not Faith

Introduction
Religion, in its purest and oldest form, is a path‚Äîa metaphysical system designed to restore the human being to a state of coherence, wholeness, or alignment with something greater than themselves. It teaches that all men are born whole, but not holy; that while the human form is complete in its design, the soul‚Äôs development requires practice, sacrifice, and conscious effort. Holiness is not inherited‚Äîit is cultivated.

Judaism breaks this structure. It does not teach that wholeness must be protected or recovered. It teaches that a select group of people are born chosen, born spiritually distinct, and that their divine status is not based on choice, belief, or righteousness‚Äîbut on blood. In doing so, Judaism forfeits its right to be called a religion in the classical sense. It becomes something else entirely: a closed metaphysical caste system, built on bloodline supremacy, legal dualism, and ideological immunity from criticism.

This essay outlines the totality of that contradiction‚Äîtracing its implications through history, law, theology, ethics, and civilization.

1. The Definition of Religion and Its Violation

A true religion is based on the following philosophical principles:

Universality of moral law: all humans are subject to the same ethical expectations.
Access through belief and transformation: anyone, through conviction and practice, can attain spiritual proximity to the divine.
Inner growth over outer identity: holiness is something acquired through humility and discipline‚Äînot inherited.
Judaism violates every one of these principles:

Jewish law contains dual moral standards‚Äîone for Jews, another for non-Jews.
Jewish identity is based on matrilineal descent, not belief. A secular Jew born of a Jewish mother is fully included; a devout non-Jew is not.
Conversion is discouraged or obstructed, and converts are frequently treated as second-tier members, with limited status in communal or legal life.
Thus, Judaism is not a religion in the universal sense. It is a tribal legal system that functions to preserve a bloodline, not liberate souls.

2. Bloodline as Theology: The Root of Supremacy

Judaism is the only major system of belief that makes bloodline the foundation of divine favor:

The concept of being ‚Äúchosen‚Äù is not metaphorical‚Äîit is ethnic.
Jewish souls are taught in mystical texts (e.g., the Tanya) to be of different spiritual origin than non-Jews.
Jewish law privileges the in-group at all levels‚Äîlegal exemptions, ritual obligations, and even in matters of debt, war, and property.
‚ÄúYou may charge a foreigner interest, but not your brother Israelite.‚Äù ‚Äî Deuteronomy 23:20
This passage encodes a moral double standard directly into the divine law, based not on behavior, but on kinship. There is no teaching here that all men must be treated with equal love, or that righteousness determines moral standing. What matters is who your mother is.

This is not religion. It is sacred tribalism, and its goal is not spiritual refinement‚Äîbut preservation of the tribe‚Äôs dominance and exemption from universal standards.

3. Judaism‚Äôs Obsession With Bloodline: A Uniquely Racial Doctrine

In all other world religions, race plays no structural role:

There are black Christians, white Muslims, Chinese Buddhists, and all are considered spiritually equal.
None of these systems require DNA testing to verify the legitimacy of one‚Äôs faith.
Judaism stands alone in:

Using genetic testing (e.g., for Israeli immigration or tribal lineage).
Prioritizing matrilineal descent over belief or behavior.
Structuring religious law and community access around ancestral identity, not theological adherence.
The consequence is that Judaism treats non-Jews not as fellow seekers of truth, but as external to the divine order, even when moral, believing, or righteous.

This is not religion. It is racial exceptionalism, encoded in theology.

4. Holiness vs. Wholeness: The Metaphysical Contradiction

‚ÄúEveryone is born perfectly whole, but to die holy takes work.‚Äù
This simple statement destroys the Judaic model. For in Judaism, the soul of the Jew is:

Born holy by default, requiring maintenance, not transformation.
Not subject to metaphysical decay, only legal infractions.
Different in kind from that of the non-Jew‚Äîwho is seen as spiritually limited or incomplete.
True religions teach that:

Holiness is earned through the struggle to stay coherent with the source of being.
Degeneration is always possible; no one is exempt.
The soul must be restored daily through practice, repentance, and humility.
Judaism‚Äôs denial of that struggle creates an ethnic aristocracy of the spirit, where no internal evolution is required. This is not a faith‚Äîit is spiritual entitlement.

5. The Immaculate Conception as Rebuttal to Tribal Bloodlines

The Christian doctrine of the Virgin Birth is not merely mystical‚Äîit is a direct metaphysical response to the Judaic bloodline system:

Jesus is born without male lineage, severing tribal inheritance.
His authority comes from divine truth, not genetic descent.
His message is open to all, and holiness is accessible by faith and moral rebirth, not ancestry.
The Jewish response was not to engage this transformation, but to reinforce the racial narrative by shifting from patrilineal to matrilineal descent, protecting their exclusivity against the rising tide of universal faith.

Thus, Christianity and Judaism are not merely different religions. They are opposite metaphysical systems:

One says ‚ÄúCome as you are, be transformed.‚Äù
The other says ‚ÄúOnly we are chosen, and you never will be.‚Äù
6. The Holocaust as Sacred Immunity: Narrative Weaponization

The Holocaust, while a real and horrifying event, has been elevated into a civilizational firewall around Judaism:

Criticism of Jewish power, law, or exclusivity is labeled ‚Äúantisemitism.‚Äù
‚ÄúAntisemitism‚Äù is treated as an eternal, irrational force, divorced from behavior, history, or structure.
Holocaust memorialization has become a global moral economy, used to:
Justify Zionism
Criminalize dissent
Claim perpetual moral superiority
Meanwhile, no other ethnic group‚Äîdespite greater poverty, higher violence rates, or comparable genocides‚Äîhas the same institutionalized protection of their trauma.

7. The Myth of Jewish Victimhood in the Modern Era

When we examine actual violence, Jews are among the safest, most protected groups in the world:

They experience far fewer murders than Blacks, Latinos, Asians, or even white working-class communities.
Most ‚Äúantisemitic‚Äù incidents reported are graffiti, jokes, or speech‚Äînot physical attacks.
Yet Jews are uniquely protected in law, media, and public discourse.
The idea that Jews are uniquely endangered is not supported by data. It is a cultural construct‚Äîmaintained to shield a system that elevates one people‚Äôs identity, law, and history above all others.

‚ÄúIf doctors were just like astrologers (in fact they were normally one and the same people) in having available numerous tactics for explaining away failure, medicine was rather different from astrology in that it appeared to give patients control over their own fate. ‚ÄúDissection for Aristotle was thus the study of function. He looked inside the bodies of animals to find out what each part did. And what he found was evidence of craftsmanship.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúWhat was new in Aristotle was his conviction that every part of the body fulfilled a function: Aristotle invented the notion that the body is made of organs (the word originally means ‚Äòtools‚Äô) that are designed to serve a purpose. ‚ÄúGalen called the non-naturals, contrasting them to the naturals (over which we have no control‚Äî climate, season, age, sex, etc.) and the unnaturals (those conditions that were directly associated with disease). The non-naturals were food and drink, the environment (e.g. exposure to the air), sleep and waking, exercise and rest, evacuations (including sexual), the passions and emotions. A major difference between medicine in the time of Galen and medicine six centuries earlier, in the time of Hippocrates, was that Galen was convinced that we could fundamentally control the conditions needed for health; this involved sharply downplaying the role of climate and season compared to the views expressed by the Hippocratic writers, for whom a change in the direction of the wind had been sufficient to explain an outbreak of illness‚ÄîSydenham‚Äôs wish to link diseases to times and places represented a return to Hippocrates and a rejection of Galen.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúTo achieve a healthy body and disposition you thus needed to counteract the effects of the climate and the season‚Äîin summer, and in Germany, you would want to cool your blood; in winter, and in Africa, to heat it.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúWhatever internal heat they have has retreated, along with the blood, into the internal organs; and there the blood churns about, confined in a small space, and boils; and thus they become spirited, bold, and quick-tempered.‚Äù

 

‚Äúongst the first Hippocratics, and disagreement remained commonplace throughout the classical period. It is not until 1200 that a single body of medical doctrine established an unchallenged predominance both in Islam and in Christendom. Some early doctors, including perhaps Hippocrates himself, believed that health and disease were all a matter of pneuma or vital spirit. Others were particularly concerned with two fluids that they believed were pernicious in excess, phlegm, which was particularly problematic in the winter, and associated with upper respiratory tract problems, and bile, which was particularly problematic in the summer, and associated with stomach problems, and they sought to explain other diseases with reference to these fluids: epilepsy, for example, was held to be brought on by phlegm. Around the time of Hippocrates‚Äô birth a philosopher called Alcmaeon of Croton had argued that health depended on a balance or equilibrium between three sets of opposing forces‚Äîhot and cold, dry and wet, sweet and sour‚Äîwhile the supremacy of any one of them (the language he uses is political for his term for supremacy is monarchia) would cause disease‚Äù

 

‚ÄúAt the heart of this enterprise was prognosis. Doctors knew they would be blamed if patients died unless they took the precaution of announcing in advance that treatment was hopeless. They needed to know, therefore, ‚Äòfor a patient with fever to grind his teeth, unless this be a habit continued from childhood, is a sign of madness and death. If this occurs during delirium, it is a sign that the disease has already taken a fatal turn.‚Äô The preoccupation with prognosis reflected an imperfect recognition of the limits of the doctor‚Äôs capacity to intervene. Galen (AD 131‚Äì201) said prognosis properly included the subdisciplines of diagnosis (identifying the patient‚Äôs present condition) and a form of mnemonics (identifying the patient‚Äôs past conditions); but both were subsidiary to prognosis. Skill at prognosis, in traditional Hippocratic/Galenic medicine, in fact served as a substitute for skill in therapy. Hippocratic doctors were able to reliably identify the signs of imminent death. But they could do nothing to delay its arrival.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúFirst we must consider the nature of man in general and of each individual and the characteristics of each disease. Then we must consider the patient, what food is given to him and who gives it‚Äîfor this may make it easier for him to take or more difficult‚Äîthe conditions of climate or locality both in general and in particular, the patient‚Äôs customs, mode of life, pursuits and age. Then we must consider his speech, his mannerisms, his silences, his thoughts, his habits of speech or wakefulness and his dreams, their nature and time. Next we must note if he plucks his hair, scratches, or weeps. We must observe his paroxysms, his stools, urine, sputum and vomit. We look for any change in the state of the malady, how often such changes occur and their nature, and the particular changes which induce death or a crisis. Observe, too, sweating, shivering, chill, cough, sneezing, hiccough, the kind of breathing, belching, wind, whether silent or noisy, hemorrhages and hemorrhoids. We must determine the significance of all these signs.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúThe first was that you should manage the conduct of daily life so that the right amount and type of food, drink, exercise, sleep and so forth encouraged health, and when disease set in you should try to counteract excess or deficiency‚Äîso they prescribed exercise for someone who rested too much, or dieting for someone who ate too much. ‚Äù

 

‚ÄúEvery part of the body which is covered by flesh or muscle contains a cavity. Every separate organ, whether covered by skin or muscle, is hollow, and in health is filled with life-giving spirit; in sickness it is pervaded by unhealthy humours. The arms, for example, possess such a cavity, as do the thighs and legs. Even those parts which are relatively poorly covered with flesh contain such cavities. Thus the trunk is hollow and contains the liver, the skull contains the brain and the thorax the lungs. Thus the divisions of the body may be likened to a series of vessels, each containing within it various organs, some of which are harmful and some beneficial to their possessor.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúWhy did it take fifty years to invent anaesthesia?‚Äù

 

‚ÄúThink for a moment what surgery was like before the invention of anaesthesia in 1842. Imagine amputating the limb of a patient who is screaming and struggling. Imagine training yourself to be indifferent to the patient‚Äôs suffering, to be deaf to their screams. Imagine developing the strength to pin down the patient‚Äôs thrashing body. Imagine learning how to be, as Ambroise Par√©, the great sixteenth-century surgeon who pioneered the tying off of blood vessels when performing amputations, put it, ‚Äòresolute and merciless‚Äô. Imagine taking pride, above all, in the speed with which you wield the knife, in never having to pause for thought or breath: speed was essential, for the shock of an operation could itself be a major factor in bringing about the patient‚Äôs death.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúBut the more I looked for the logic of discovery, the more often it seemed to slip through my fingers.‚Äù

 

‚Äúyet in 1820 microscopes had no place in medical research, and in 1881 the conflict between germ theorists and their opponents was only just entering its final phase. Penicillin was first discovered not in 1941 but in 1872. And so on.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúOnce Pasteur had discovered a vaccine for anthrax in 1881, the hunt for other vaccines was on. Once penicillin had been discovered in 1941, the hunt for other antibiotics was on.‚Äù

 

‚ÄúAnd then I slowly became aware of a third problem. Histories of progress are written on the assumption that there is a logic of discovery. Once you discover Œ± (say, germs), it is easy to discover Œ≤ (say, antibiotics); without a theory of germs you will never discover antibiotics. A good example is Newton‚Äôs‚Äù

 

‚ÄúThe idea that progress in knowledge and progress in therapy are quite distinct may seem an obvious point, but it took me a long while to grasp it. When I started working on this book, my intention was to write a history of different ways of conceiving of the human body‚Äî in terms of the four humours (ancient and medieval medicine); as a mechanical system in which the heart functions as a bellows (the medicine of the scientific revolution); as a system of chemical interactions (nineteenth-century medicine); as a system for the replication of genes (twentieth-century medicine), and so forth. Each represented itself as an advance on its predecessors.‚Äù

provide three points from the book that Wootton uses to assert  each of the following claims: Medicine as Ritual Performance
For much of human history, medical encounters were about reassurance, control, and social order‚Äînot healing. Doctors were akin to priests, and medicine a secular liturgy.
The Placebo and the Illusion of Efficacy
Treatments were often effective only in the sense that belief in them alleviated symptoms. The placebo effect was retrofitted into modern understandings but had always functioned implicitly.
Patient Perception as Central
Wootton gives weight to the patient‚Äôs experience‚Äîsomething rarely emphasized in traditional histories of medicine. Healing was often judged subjectively, which allowed ineffective treatments to flourish.
No Lineage Between Ancient and Modern Medicine
Wootton dismantles the heroic continuity myth. Modern medicine is not the apex of an unbroken tradition but a rupture‚Äîa radical departure from what came before.
Legal, Social, and Religious Legitimacy Over Empirical Proof
Medicine gained its legitimacy not through its results but through state authorization and religious analogy. This is why untested or even harmful treatments persisted.
Progress as Contingent, Not Cumulative
Discoveries like penicillin, anaesthesia, and vaccines occurred not because of medicine‚Äôs internal logic but often despite it. There was no inevitability‚Äîjust historical luck, individual brilliance, and finally the breakdown of old frameworks.

assess the text. are there any statements we could use as a basis of a vaccine court case in addition to all the recent studies finding covid vaccine harm and the CDC walking back their covid vaccine recommendations?

The Secretary shall establish in the Department of Health and Human Services a National Vaccine Program to achieve optimal prevention of human infectious diseases through immunization and to achieve optimal prevention against adverse reactions to vaccines. The Program shall be administered by a Director selected by the Secretary.

Study of Other Vaccine Risks
Pub. L. 99‚Äì660, title III, ¬ß313, Nov. 14, 1986, 100 Stat. 3781, provided that:

"(a) Study.‚Äî

"(1) Not later than 3 years after the effective date of this title [see Effective Date note above], the Secretary shall, after consultation with the Advisory Commission on Childhood Vaccines established under section 2119 of the Public Health Service Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì19]‚Äî

"(A) arrange for a broad study of the risks (other than the risks considered under section 102 [21 U.S.C. 382]) to children associated with each vaccine set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table under section 2114 of such Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì14], and

"(B) establish guidelines, after notice and opportunity for public hearing and consideration of all relevant medical and scientific information, respecting the administration of such vaccines which shall include‚Äî

"(i) the circumstances under which any such vaccine should not be administered,

"(ii) the circumstances under which administration of any such vaccine should be delayed beyond its usual time of administration, and

"(iii) the groups, categories, or characteristics of potential recipients of such vaccine who may be at significantly higher risk of major adverse reactions to such vaccine than the general population of potential recipients.

"(2)(A) The Secretary shall request the Institute of Medicine of the National Academy of Sciences to conduct the study required by paragraph (1) under an arrangement by which the actual expenses incurred by such Academy in conducting such study will be paid by the Secretary.

"(B) If the Institute of Medicine is unwilling to conduct such study under such an arrangement, the Secretary shall enter into a similar arrangement with other appropriate nonprofit private groups or associations under which such groups or associations will conduct such study.

"(C) The Institute of Medicine or other group or association conducting the study required by paragraph (1) shall conduct such studies in consultation with the Advisory Commission on Childhood Vaccines established under section 2119 of the Public Health Service Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì19].

"(b) Revision of Guidelines.‚ÄîThe Secretary shall periodically, but at least every 3 years after establishing guidelines under subsection (a), review and revise such guidelines after notice and opportunity for public hearing and consideration of all relevant medical and scientific information, unless the Secretary finds that on the basis of all relevant information no revision of such guidelines is warranted and publishes such finding in the Federal Register.

"(c) Factors Affecting Guidelines.‚ÄîGuidelines under subsection (a) shall take into account‚Äî

"(1) the risk to potential recipients of the vaccines with respect to which the guidelines are established,

"(2) the medical and other characteristics of such potential recipients, and

"(3) the risks to the public of not having such vaccines administered.

"(d) Dissemination.‚ÄîThe Secretary shall widely disseminate the guidelines established under subsection (a) to‚Äî

"(1) physicians and other health care providers,

"(2) professional health associations,

"(3) State and local governments and agencies, and

"(4) other relevant entities."

L. 99‚Äì660, title III, ¬ß314, Nov. 14, 1986, 100 Stat. 3782, directed Secretary of Health and Human Services, not later than 1 year after the effective date of this title (see Effective Date note above) and after consultation with Advisory Commission on Childhood Vaccines and with other appropriate entities, to review the warnings, use instructions, and precautionary information presently issued by manufacturers of vaccines set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table set out in section 300aa‚Äì14 of this title and by rule determine whether such warnings, instructions, and information adequately warn health care providers of the nature and extent of dangers posed by such vaccines, and, if any such warning, instruction, or information is determined to be inadequate for such purpose in any respect, require at the same time that the manufacturers revise and reissue such warning, instruction, or information as expeditiously as practical, but not later than 18 months after the effective date of this title.

¬ß300aa‚Äì2. Program responsibilities
(a) The Director of the Program shall have the following responsibilities:

(1) Vaccine research
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, coordinate and provide direction for research carried out in or through the National Institutes of Health, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, the Office of Biologics Research and Review of the Food and Drug Administration, the Department of Defense, and the Agency for International Development on means to induce human immunity against naturally occurring infectious diseases and to prevent adverse reactions to vaccines.

(2) Vaccine development
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, coordinate and provide direction for activities carried out in or through the National Institutes of Health, the Office of Biologics Research and Review of the Food and Drug Administration, the Department of Defense, and the Agency for International Development to develop the techniques needed to produce safe and effective vaccines.

(3) Safety and efficacy testing of vaccines
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, coordinate and provide direction for safety and efficacy testing of vaccines carried out in or through the National Institutes of Health, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, the Office of Biologics Research and Review of the Food and Drug Administration, the Department of Defense, and the Agency for International Development.

(4) Licensing of vaccine manufacturers and vaccines
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, coordinate and provide direction for the allocation of resources in the implementation of the licensing program under section 263a of this title.

(5) Production and procurement of vaccines
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, ensure that the governmental and non-governmental production and procurement of safe and effective vaccines by the Public Health Service, the Department of Defense, and the Agency for International Development meet the needs of the United States population and fulfill commitments of the United States to prevent human infectious diseases in other countries.

(6) Distribution and use of vaccines
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, coordinate and provide direction to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention and assistance to States, localities, and health practitioners in the distribution and use of vaccines, including efforts to encourage public acceptance of immunizations and to make health practitioners and the public aware of potential adverse reactions and contraindications to vaccines.

(7) Evaluating the need for and the effectiveness and adverse effects of vaccines and immunization activities
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, coordinate and provide direction to the National Institutes of Health, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, the Office of Biologics Research and Review of the Food and Drug Administration, the National Center for Health Statistics, the National Center for Health Services Research and Health Care Technology Assessment, and the Centers for Medicare & Medicaid Services in monitoring the need for and the effectiveness and adverse effects of vaccines and immunization activities.

(8) Coordinating governmental and non-governmental activities
The Director of the Program shall, through the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, provide for the exchange of information between Federal agencies involved in the implementation of the Program and non-governmental entities engaged in the development and production of vaccines and in vaccine research and encourage the investment of non-governmental resources complementary to the governmental activities under the Program.

(9) Funding of Federal agencies
The Director of the Program shall make available to Federal agencies involved in the implementation of the plan issued under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title funds appropriated under section 300aa‚Äì6 of this title to supplement the funds otherwise available to such agencies for activities under the plan.


(b) In carrying out subsection (a) and in preparing the plan under section 300aa‚Äì3 of this title, the Director shall consult with all Federal agencies involved in research on and development, testing, licensing, production, procurement, distribution, and use of vaccines.

(July 1, 1944, ch. 373, title XXI, ¬ß2102, as added Pub. L. 99‚Äì660, title III, ¬ß311(a), Nov. 14, 1986, 100 Stat. 3756; amended Pub. L. 102‚Äì531, title III, ¬ß312(d)(13), Oct. 27, 1992, 106 Stat. 3505; Pub. L. 108‚Äì173, title IX, ¬ß900(e)(2)(F), Dec. 8, 2003, 117 Stat. 2372.)

¬ß300aa‚Äì3. Plan
The Director of the Program shall prepare and issue a plan for the implementation of the responsibilities of the Director under section 300aa‚Äì2 of this title. The plan shall establish priorities in research and the development, testing, licensing, production, procurement, distribution, and effective use of vaccines, describe an optimal use of resources to carry out such priorities, and describe how each of the various departments and agencies will carry out their vaccine functions in consultation and coordination with the Program and in conformity with such priorities. The first plan under this section shall be prepared not later than January 1, 1987, and shall be revised not later than January 1 of each succeeding year.

(July 1, 1944, ch. 373, title XXI, ¬ß2103, as added Pub. L. 99‚Äì660, title III, ¬ß311(a), Nov. 14, 1986, 100 Stat. 3757.)

¬ß300aa‚Äì5. National Vaccine Advisory Committee
(a) There is established the National Vaccine Advisory Committee. The members of the Committee shall be appointed by the Director of the Program, in consultation with the National Academy of Sciences, from among individuals who are engaged in vaccine research or the manufacture of vaccines or who are physicians, members of parent organizations concerned with immunizations, or representatives of State or local health agencies or public health organizations.

(b) The Committee shall‚Äî

(1) study and recommend ways to encourage the availability of an adequate supply of safe and effective vaccination products in the States,

(2) recommend research priorities and other measures the Director of the Program should take to enhance the safety and efficacy of vaccines,

(3) advise the Director of the Program in the implementation of sections 300aa‚Äì2, 300aa‚Äì3, and 300aa‚Äì4 1 of this title, and

(4) identify annually for the Director of the Program the most important areas of government and non-government cooperation that should be considered in implementing sections 300aa‚Äì2, 300aa‚Äì3, and 300aa‚Äì4 1 of this title.

(July 1, 1944, ch. 373, title XXI, ¬ß2105, as added Pub. L. 99‚Äì660, title III, ¬ß311(a), Nov. 14, 1986, 100 Stat. 3758.)

Part 2‚ÄîNational Vaccine Injury Compensation Program
subpart a‚Äîprogram requirements
¬ß300aa‚Äì10. Establishment of program
(a) Program established
There is established the National Vaccine Injury Compensation Program to be administered by the Secretary under which compensation may be paid for a vaccine-related injury or death.

(b) Attorney's obligation
It shall be the ethical obligation of any attorney who is consulted by an individual with respect to a vaccine-related injury or death to advise such individual that compensation may be available under the program 1 for such injury or death.

(c) Publicity
The Secretary shall undertake reasonable efforts to inform the public of the availability of the Program.

(July 1, 1944, ch. 373, title XXI, ¬ß2110, as added Pub. L. 99‚Äì660, title III, ¬ß311(a), Nov. 14, 1986, 100 Stat. 3758; amended Pub. L. 101‚Äì239, title VI, ¬ß6601(b), Dec. 19, 1989, 103 Stat. 2285.)

Effective Date of 1989 Amendment
Section 6601(s) of Pub. L. 101‚Äì239, as amended by Pub. L. 102‚Äì572, title IX, ¬ß902(b)(1), Oct. 29, 1992, 106 Stat. 4516, provided that:

"(1) Except as provided in paragraph (2), the amendments made by this section [amending this section and sections 300aa‚Äì11 to 300aa‚Äì17, 300aa‚Äì21, 300aa‚Äì23, 300aa‚Äì26, and 300aa‚Äì27 of this title] shall apply as follows:

"(A) Petitions filed after the date of enactment of this section [Dec. 19, 1989] shall proceed under the National Vaccine Injury Compensation Program under title XXI of the Public Health Service Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì1 et seq.] as amended by this section.

"(B) Petitions currently pending in which the evidentiary record is closed shall continue to proceed under the Program in accordance with the law in effect before the date of the enactment of this section, except that if the United States Court of Federal Claims is to review the findings of fact and conclusions of law of a special master on such a petition, the court may receive further evidence in conducting such review.

"(C) Petitions currently pending in which the evidentiary record is not closed shall proceed under the Program in accordance with the law as amended by this section.

All pending cases which will proceed under the Program as amended by this section shall be immediately suspended for 30 days to enable the special masters and parties to prepare for proceeding under the Program as amended by this section. In determining the 240-day period prescribed by section 2112(d) of the Public Health Service Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì12(d)], as amended by this section, or the 420-day period prescribed by section 2121(b) of such Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì21(b)], as so amended, any period of suspension under the preceding sentence shall be excluded.

"(2) The amendments to section 2115 of the Public Health Service Act [42 U.S.C. 300aa‚Äì15] shall apply to all pending and subsequently filed petitions."

¬ß300aa‚Äì11. Petitions for compensation
(a) General rule
(1) A proceeding for compensation under the Program for a vaccine-related injury or death shall be initiated by service upon the Secretary and the filing of a petition containing the matter prescribed by subsection (c) with the United States Court of Federal Claims. The clerk of the United States Court of Federal Claims shall immediately forward the filed petition to the chief special master for assignment to a special master under section 300aa‚Äì12(d)(1) of this title.

(2)(A) No person may bring a civil action for damages in an amount greater than $1,000 or in an unspecified amount against a vaccine administrator or manufacturer in a State or Federal court for damages arising from a vaccine-related injury or death associated with the administration of a vaccine after October 1, 1988, and no such court may award damages in an amount greater than $1,000 in a civil action for damages for such a vaccine-related injury or death, unless a petition has been filed, in accordance with section 300aa‚Äì16 of this title, for compensation under the Program for such injury or death and‚Äî

(i)(I) the United States Court of Federal Claims has issued a judgment under section 300aa‚Äì12 of this title on such petition, and

(II) such person elects under section 300aa‚Äì21(a) of this title to file such an action, or

(ii) such person elects to withdraw such petition under section 300aa‚Äì21(b) of this title or such petition is considered withdrawn under such section.


(B) If a civil action which is barred under subparagraph (A) is filed in a State or Federal court, the court shall dismiss the action. If a petition is filed under this section with respect to the injury or death for which such civil action was brought, the date such dismissed action was filed shall, for purposes of the limitations of actions prescribed by section 300aa‚Äì16 of this title, be considered the date the petition was filed if the petition was filed within one year of the date of the dismissal of the civil action.

(3) No vaccine administrator or manufacturer may be made a party to a civil action (other than a civil action which may be brought under paragraph (2)) for damages for a vaccine-related injury or death associated with the administration of a vaccine after October 1, 1988.

(4) If in a civil action brought against a vaccine administrator or manufacturer before October 1, 1988, damages were denied for a vaccine-related injury or death or if such civil action was dismissed with prejudice, the person who brought such action may file a petition under subsection (b) for such injury or death.

(5)(A) A plaintiff who on October 1, 1988, has pending a civil action for damages for a vaccine-related injury or death may, at any time within 2 years after October 1, 1988, or before judgment, whichever occurs first, petition to have such action dismissed without prejudice or costs and file a petition under subsection (b) for such injury or death.

(B) If a plaintiff has pending a civil action for damages for a vaccine-related injury or death, such person may not file a petition under subsection (b) for such injury or death.

(6) If a person brings a civil action after November 15, 1988 for damages for a vaccine-related injury or death associated with the administration of a vaccine before November 15, 1988, such person may not file a petition under subsection (b) for such injury or death.

(7) If in a civil action brought against a vaccine administrator or manufacturer for a vaccine-related injury or death damages are awarded under a judgment of a court or a settlement of such action, the person who brought such action may not file a petition under subsection (b) for such injury or death.

(8) If on October 1, 1988, there was pending an appeal or rehearing with respect to a civil action brought against a vaccine administrator or manufacturer and if the outcome of the last appellate review of such action or the last rehearing of such action is the denial of damages for a vaccine-related injury or death, the person who brought such action may file a petition under subsection (b) for such injury or death.

(9) This subsection applies only to a person who has sustained a vaccine-related injury or death and who is qualified to file a petition for compensation under the Program.

(10) The Clerk of the United States Claims Court 1 is authorized to continue to receive, and forward, petitions for compensation for a vaccine-related injury or death associated with the administration of a vaccine on or after October 1, 1992.

(b) Petitioners
(1)(A) Except as provided in subparagraph (B), any person who has sustained a vaccine-related injury, the legal representative of such person if such person is a minor or is disabled, or the legal representative of any person who died as the result of the administration of a vaccine set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table may, if the person meets the requirements of subsection (c)(1), file a petition for compensation under the Program.

(B) No person may file a petition for a vaccine-related injury or death associated with a vaccine administered before October 1, 1988, if compensation has been paid under this part for 3500 petitions for such injuries or deaths.

(2) Only one petition may be filed with respect to each administration of a vaccine. A covered vaccine administered to a pregnant woman shall constitute more than one administration, one to the mother and one to each child (as such term is defined in subsection (f)(2)) who was in utero at the time such woman was administered the vaccine.

(c) Petition content
A petition for compensation under the Program for a vaccine-related injury or death shall contain‚Äî

(1) except as provided in paragraph (3), an affidavit, and supporting documentation, demonstrating that the person who suffered such injury or who died‚Äî

(A) received a vaccine set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table or, if such person did not receive such a vaccine, contracted polio, directly or indirectly, from another person who received an oral polio vaccine,

(B)(i) if such person received a vaccine set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table‚Äî

(I) received the vaccine in the United States or in its trust territories,

(II) received the vaccine outside the United States or a trust territory and at the time of the vaccination such person was a citizen of the United States serving abroad as a member of the Armed Forces or otherwise as an employee of the United States or a dependent of such a citizen, or

(III) received the vaccine outside the United States or a trust territory and the vaccine was manufactured by a vaccine manufacturer located in the United States and such person returned to the United States not later than 6 months after the date of the vaccination,


(ii) if such person did not receive such a vaccine but contracted polio from another person who received an oral polio vaccine, was a citizen of the United States or a dependent of such a citizen,

(C)(i) sustained, or had significantly aggravated, any illness, disability, injury, or condition set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table in association with the vaccine referred to in subparagraph (A) or died from the administration of such vaccine, and the first symptom or manifestation of the onset or of the significant aggravation of any such illness, disability, injury, or condition or the death occurred within the time period after vaccine administration set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table, or

(ii)(I) sustained, or had significantly aggravated, any illness, disability, injury, or condition not set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table but which was caused by a vaccine referred to in subparagraph (A), or

(II) sustained, or had significantly aggravated, any illness, disability, injury, or condition set forth in the Vaccine Injury Table the first symptom or manifestation of the onset or significant aggravation of which did not occur within the time period set forth in the Table but which was caused by a vaccine referred to in subparagraph (A),

(D)(i) suffered the residual effects or complications of such illness, disability, injury, or condition for more than 6 months after the administration of the vaccine, or (ii) died from the administration of the vaccine, or (iii) suffered such illness, disability, injury, or condition from the vaccine which resulted in inpatient hospitalization and surgical intervention, and

(E) has not previously collected an award or settlement of a civil action for damages for such vaccine-related injury or death,


(2) except as provided in paragraph (3), maternal prenatal and delivery records, newborn hospital records (including all physicians' and nurses' notes and test results), vaccination records associated with the vaccine allegedly causing the injury, pre- and post-injury physician or clinic records (including all relevant growth charts and test results), all post-injury inpatient and outpatient records (including all provider notes, test results, and medication records), if applicable, a death certificate, and if applicable, autopsy results, and

(3) an identification of any records of the type described in paragraph (1) or (2) which are unavailable to the petitioner and the reasons for their unavailability.

(d) Additional information
A petition may also include other available relevant medical records relating to the person who suffered such injury or who died from the administration of the vaccine.

(e) Schedule
The petitioner shall submit in accordance with a schedule set by the special master assigned to the petition assessments, evaluations, and prognoses and such other records and documents as are reasonably necessary for the determination of the amount of compensation to be paid to, or on behalf of, the person who suffered such injury or who died from the administration of the vaccine.

(f) Maternal immunization
(1) In general
Notwithstanding any other provision of law, for purposes of this subpart, both a woman who received a covered vaccine while pregnant and any child who was in utero at the time such woman received the vaccine shall be considered persons to whom the covered vaccine was administered and persons who received the covered vaccine.

(2) Definition
As used in this subsection, the term "child" shall have the meaning given that term by subsections (a) and (b) of section 8 of title 1 except that, for purposes of this subsection, such section 8 shall be applied as if the term "include" in subsection (a) of such section were replaced with the term "mean".
